href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>l<b>a</b>me, r<b>ei</b>n, st<b>ai</b>n\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xc9\xaa/</span>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x94\xc9\xaa]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x99\xc9\xaa~\xc9\x91\xc9\xaa]\n</td>\n<td>[a\xc9\xaa~\xc3\xa4\xc9\xaa]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x92\xc9\xaa~o\xc9\xaa]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x92\xc9\xaa]\n</td>\n<td>b<b>oy</b>, ch<b>oi</b>ce, m<b>oi</b>st\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/o\xca\x8a/</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[o\xcb\x90]\n</td>\n<td>[\xca\x8co~\xca\x8c\xc9\x94]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x99\xca\x8a]\n</td>\n<td>[o\xca\x8a~\xc9\x99\xca\x8a]\n</td>\n<td>g<b>oa</b>t, <b>oh</b>, sh<b>ow</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Footnotes:\'</b>\n<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_vowelbreak"><b><a href="#ref_vowelbreak">^1</a></b></span>  \nDue to the local Dublin accent\'s phenomenon of "vowel breaking", <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xc9\xaa/</span> may be realised in that accent as [\xc9\x99j\xc9\x99] in a <a href="/wiki/Closed_syllable" class="mw-redirect" title="Closed syllable">closed syllable</a>, and, in the same environment, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xca\x8a/</span> may be realised as [\xc9\x9bw\xc9\x99].\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="R-coloured_vowels"><i>R</i>-coloured vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: R-coloured vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>The defining <a href="/wiki/R-coloured_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="R-coloured vowel"><i>r</i>-coloured vowels</a> of Hiberno-English:</b>\n</p><p>The following <i>r</i>-coloured vowel features are defining characteristics of Hiberno-English: \n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">Rhoticity</a>: Every major accent of Hiberno-English pronounces the letter "r" whenever it follows a vowel sound, though this is weaker in the local Dublin accent due to its earlier history of non-rhoticity. Rhoticity is a feature that Hiberno-English shares with <a href="/wiki/Canadian_English" title="Canadian English">Canadian English</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> but not with <a href="/wiki/Received_Pronunciation" title="Received Pronunciation">Received Pronunciation</a>.</li>\n<li>The distinction between <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90r/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/o\xca\x8ar/</span> is almost always preserved, so that, for example, <i>horse</i> and <i>hoarse</i> are not <a href="/wiki/Horse%E2%80%93hoarse_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Horse\xe2\x80\x93hoarse merger">merged</a> in most Irish accents.</li></ul>\n<p><b>All <i>r</i>-coloured vowels of various Hiberno-English dialects:</b>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<caption>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><b>English <br />diaphoneme</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Ulster</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>West &amp; <br />South-West Ireland</b><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_old"><a href="#endnote_old">1</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><b>Local <br />Dublin</b><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_old"><a href="#endnote_old">1</a></sup><sup>,</sup> <sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_rhotic"><a href="#endnote_rhotic">2</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><b>New <br />Dublin</b><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_NLD"><a href="#endnote_NLD">3</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><b>Supraregional <br />Ireland</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Example words</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90r/</span>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x91\xc9\xbb~\xc9\x91\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xc3\xa6\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~a\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xc3\xa4\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~\xc9\x91\xc9\xb9]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_D4"><a href="#endnote_D4">4</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>c<b>ar</b>, g<b>uar</b>d, p<b>ar</b>k\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\xaa\xc9\x99r/</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="5">[i\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~i\xc9\x9a]\n</td>\n<td>f<b>ear</b>, p<b>eer</b>, t<b>ier</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x9b\xc9\x99r/</span>\n</td>\n<td>[(\xc9\x9b)\xc9\x9a\xcb\x90]\n</td>\n<td colspan="4">[\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~e\xc9\xb9]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_ND"><a href="#endnote_ND">5</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>b<b>are</b>, b<b>ear</b>, th<b>ere</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90r/</span><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_er"><a href="#endnote_er">6</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x9a\xcb\x90]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9] or [\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_er"><a href="#endnote_er">6</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xc9\x9a\xcb\x90]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_ND"><a href="#endnote_ND">5</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>b<b>ur</b>n, f<b>ir</b>st, l<b>ear</b>n\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99r/</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="5">[\xc9\x9a]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_schwa-r"><a href="#endnote_schwa-r">7</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>doct<b>or</b>, mart<b>yr</b>, p<b>er</b>vade\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90r/</span><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_horse"><a href="#endnote_horse">8</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x92\xcb\x90\xc9\x9a~\xc9\x94\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xc3\xa4\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~\xc9\x91\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xc9\x92\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~o\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td>f<b>or</b>, h<b>or</b>se, w<b>ar</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>[o\xcb\x90\xc9\x9a~o\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x94\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\x92\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[o\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]\n</td>\n<td>f<b>our</b>, h<b>oar</b>se, w<b>ore</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xca\x8a\xc9\x99r/</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="5">[u\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~u\xc9\x9a]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_vowelbreak"><a href="#endnote_vowelbreak">9</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>m<b>oor</b>, p<b>oor</b>, t<b>our</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j\xca\x8a\xc9\x99r/</span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="5">[ju\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9~ju\xc9\x9a]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_vowelbreak"><a href="#endnote_vowelbreak">9</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>c<b>ure</b>, <b>Eur</b>ope, p<b>ure</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Footnotes:</b>\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_old"><b><a href="#ref_old">^1</a></b></span>  In older varieties of the conservative accents, like local Dublin, the "r" sound before a vowel may be pronounced as a <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_tap" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar tap">tapped</a> [\xc9\xbe], rather than as the typical approximant [\xc9\xb9\xcc\xa0].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_rhotic"><b><a href="#ref_rhotic">^2</a></b></span> Every major accent of Irish English is <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_and_non-rhotic_accents" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhotic and non-rhotic accents">rhotic</a> (pronounces "r" after a vowel sound). The local Dublin accent is the only one that during an earlier time was non-rhotic, though it usually very lightly rhotic today,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> with a few minor exceptions. The rhotic consonant in this and most other Irish accents is an <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar approximant">approximant</a> [\xc9\xb9\xcc\xa0].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_NLD"><b><a href="#ref_NLD">^3</a></b></span> The "r" sound of the mainstream non-local Dublin accent is more precisely a <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarised</a> <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar approximant">approximant</a> [\xc9\xb9\xcb\xa0], while the  "r" sound of the more recently emerging non-local Dublin (or "new Dublin") accent is more precisely a <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_approximant" title="Retroflex approximant">retroflex approximant</a> [\xc9\xbb].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_D4"><b><a href="#ref_D4">^4</a></b></span> In <a href="/wiki/Southside_Dublin" class="mw-redirect" title="Southside Dublin">southside Dublin</a>\'s once-briefly fashionable "<a href="/wiki/Dublin_4" title="Dublin 4">Dublin 4</a>" (or "Dortspeak") accent, /\xc9\x91r/ is realised as [\xc9\x92\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_ND"><b><a href="#ref_ND">^5</a></b></span> In non-local Dublin\'s more recently emerging (or "new Dublin") accent, /\xc9\x9b\xc9\x99r/ and /\xc9\x9cr/ may both be realised more rounded as [\xc3\xb8\xcb\x90\xc9\xbb].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_er"><b><a href="#ref_er">^6</a></b></span> In local Dublin, West/South-West, and other <a href="/wiki/Conservative_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative (linguistics)">very conservative</a> and traditional Irish English varieties ranging from the south to the north, the phoneme /\xc9\x9cr/ is split into two distinct phonemes depending on spelling and preceding consonants, which have sometimes been represented as /\xc9\x9br/ versus /\xca\x8ar/, and often more precisely pronounced as [\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9] versus [\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]. As an example, the words <i>earn</i> and <i>urn</i> are not pronounced the same, as they are in most dialects of English around the world. In the local Dublin and West/South-West accents, /\xc9\x9cr/ when after a <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">labial consonant</a> (e.g. <i>fern</i>), when spelled as "ur" or "or" (e.g. <i>word</i>), or when spelled as "ir" after an <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_stop" title="Alveolar stop">alveolar stop</a> (e.g. <i>dirt</i>) are pronounced as [\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]; in all other situations, /\xc9\x9cr/ is pronounced as [\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9].<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Example words include:\n</p>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<p><b>/\xc9\x9br/</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>certain</i> [\xcb\x88s\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9tn\xcc\xa9]</li>\n<li><i>chirp</i> [\xcb\x88t\xca\x83\xca\xb0\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9p]</li>\n<li><i>circle</i> [\xcb\x88s\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9k\xc9\x99l]</li>\n<li><i>earn</i> [\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9n]</li>\n<li><i>earth</i> [\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9t]</li>\n<li><i>girl</i> [g\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9l]</li>\n<li><i>germ</i> [d\xca\x92\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9m]</li>\n<li><i>heard</i> or <i>herd</i> [h\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9d]</li>\n<li><i>irk</i> [\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9k]</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tern" title="Tern">tern</a></i> [t\xca\xb0\xc9\x9b\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9n]</li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<p><b>/\xca\x8ar/</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>bird</i> [b\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9d]</li>\n<li><i>dirt</i> [d\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9t]</li>\n<li><i>first</i> [f\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9st]</li>\n<li><i>murder</i> [\xcb\x88m\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9d\xc9\x9a]</li>\n<li><i>nurse</i> [\xcb\x88n\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9s]</li>\n<li><i>turn</i> or <i>tern</i> [t\xca\xb0\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9n]</li>\n<li><i>third</i> or <i>turd</i> [t\xca\xb0\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9d]</li>\n<li><i>urn</i> [\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9n]</li>\n<li><i>work</i> [w\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9k]</li>\n<li><i>world</i> [w\xca\x8a\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9ld].</li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>In non-local Dublin, younger, and supraregional Irish accents, this split is seldom preserved, with both of the /\xc9\x9cr/ phonemes typically merged as [\xc9\x9a\xcb\x90].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_schwa-r"><b><a href="#ref_schwa-r">^7</a></b></span> In rare few local Dublin varieties that are non-rhotic, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99r/</span> is either lowered to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x90]</span> or backed and raised to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa4]</span>.\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_horse"><b><a href="#ref_horse">^8</a></b></span> The distinction between <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90r/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/o\xca\x8ar/</span> is widely preserved in Ireland, so that, for example, <i>horse</i> and <i>hoarse</i> are not merged in most Irish English dialects; however, they are usually <a href="/wiki/Horse%E2%80%93hoarse_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Horse\xe2\x80\x93hoarse merger">merged</a> in <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a> and new Dublin.\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_vowelbreak"><b><a href="#ref_vowelbreak">^9</a></b></span> In local Dublin, due to the phenomenon of "vowel breaking" <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[(j)u\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9]</span> may in fact be realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[(j)u\xca\xb7\xc9\x99(\xc9\xb9)]</span>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>The defining <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> of Hiberno-English:</b>\n</p><p>The consonants of Hiberno-English mostly align to the typical English consonant sounds. However, a few Irish English consonants have distinctive, varying qualities. The following consonant features are defining characteristics of Hiberno-English: \n</p>\n<ul><li><i>H</i>-fulness: Unlike most English varieties of England and Wales, which <a href="/wiki/H-dropping" title="H-dropping">drop the word-initial /h/ sound</a> in words like <i>house</i> or <i>happy</i>, Hiberno-English always retains word-initial <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span></span>/</a></span></span>.</li>\n<li>The phonemes <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xc3\xb0/: &#39;th&#39; in &#39;thy&#39;">\xc3\xb0</span></span>/</a></span></span> (as in <i>the</i>) and <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xce\xb8/: &#39;th&#39; in &#39;thigh&#39;">\xce\xb8</span></span>/</a></span></span> (as in <i>thin</i>) are pronounced uniquely in most Hiberno-English. <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xb0/</span> is pronounced as <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[d]</a></span> or <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[d\xcc\xaa]</a></span>, depending on specific dialect; and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xce\xb8/</span> is pronounced as <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[t]</a></span> or <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[t\xcc\xaa]</a></span>.</li>\n<li>The phoneme <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>, when appearing at the end of word or between vowel sounds, is pronounced uniquely in most Hiberno-English; the most common pronunciation is as a "<a href="/wiki/Slit_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Slit fricative">slit fricative</a>".</li>\n<li>The phoneme <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span></span>/</a></span></span> is almost always of a "light" or "clear" quality (i.e. not <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarised</a>), unlike Received Pronunciation, which uses both a clear and a dark "L" sound, or General American, which pronounces all "L" sounds as dark.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">Rhoticity</a>: The pronunciation of historical /r/ is nearly universal in Irish accents of English. Like with General American (but not Received Pronunciation), this means that the letter "r", if appearing after a vowel sound, is always pronounced (in words such as <i>here</i>, <i>cart</i>, or <i>surf</i>).</li></ul>\n<p><b>Unique consonants in various Hiberno-English dialects:</b>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<caption>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><b>English diaphoneme</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Ulster</b><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_NI"><a href="#endnote_NI">1</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><b>West &amp; <br />South-West Ireland</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Local <br />Dublin</b><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_Dub"><a href="#endnote_Dub">2</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><b>New<br />Dublin</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Supraregional <br />Ireland</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>Example words</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xb0/</span>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc3\xb0]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[d]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[d\xcc\xaa]\n</td>\n<td><b>th</b>is, wri<b>th</b>e, wi<b>th</b>er\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>syllable-final <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span>\n</td>\n<td>[l] or [\xc9\xab]\n</td>\n<td>[l]\n</td>\n<td colspan="3">[l] or [\xc9\xab]\n</td>\n<td>ba<b>ll</b>, so<b>l</b>dier, mi<b>l</b>k\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_r"><a href="#endnote_r">3</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xbb]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xb9\xcb\xa0]\n</td>\n<td>prevocalic/intervocalic: [\xc9\xb9\xcb\xa0] or [\xc9\xbe] <br />postvocalic: [\xe2\x88\x85] or [\xc9\xb9\xcb\xa0]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xbb]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xb9\xcb\xa0] or [\xc9\xbb]\n</td>\n<td><b>r</b>ot, shi<b>r</b>t, ta<b>r</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/ between vowels</span>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xbe], [\xca\x94], or [\xe2\x88\x85]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xbe] or [\xce\xb8\xcc\xa0]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_th"><a href="#endnote_th">4</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>[\xca\x94h]\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xbe\xce\xb8\xcc\xa0]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_th"><a href="#endnote_th">4</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>[\xc9\xbe] or [\xce\xb8\xcc\xa0]<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_th"><a href="#endnote_th">4</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>ba<b>tt</b>le, I<b>t</b>aly, wa<b>t</b>er\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/ in word-final position</span>\n</td>\n<td>[t] or [\xca\x94]\n</td>\n<td>[\xce\xb8\xcc\xa0]\n</td>\n<td>[h] or [\xe2\x88\x85]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xce\xb8\xcc\xa0]\n</td>\n<td>ca<b>t</b>, ge<b>t</b>, righ<b>t</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xce\xb8/</span>\n</td>\n<td>[\xce\xb8]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[t]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[t\xcc\xaa]\n</td>\n<td>le<b>th</b>al, <b>th</b>ick, wra<b>th</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hw/</span><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_hw"><a href="#endnote_hw">5</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>[w]\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">[\xca\x8d]\n</td>\n<td>[w]\n</td>\n<td>[\xca\x8d] or [w]\n</td>\n<td>a<b>wh</b>ile, <b>wh</b>ale, <b>wh</b>en\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Footnotes:</b>\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_NI"><b><a href="#ref_NI">^1</a></b></span> In traditional, conservative <a href="/wiki/Ulster_English" title="Ulster English">Ulster English</a>, /k/ and /g/ are <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalised</a> before a low front vowel.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Dub"><b><a href="#ref_Dub">^2</a></b></span> Local Dublin also undergoes cluster simplification, so that <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">stop consonant</a> sounds occurring after <a href="/wiki/Fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Fricative">fricatives</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorants</a> may be left unpronounced, resulting, for example, in "poun(d)" and "las(t)".<sup id="cite_ref-South-West_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South-West-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_r"><b><a href="#ref_r">^3</a></b></span> Rhoticity: Every major accent of Irish English is strongly <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_and_non-rhotic_accents" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhotic and non-rhotic accents">rhotic</a> (pronounces "r" after a vowel sound), though to a weaker degree with the local Dublin accent.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvcoltxt&#124;Hickey&#124;1984&#124;p=234_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvcoltxt|Hickey|1984|p=234-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The accents of local Dublin and some smaller eastern towns like <a href="/wiki/Drogheda" title="Drogheda">Drogheda</a> were historically non-rhotic and now only very lightly rhotic or variably rhotic, with the rhotic consonant being an alveolar approximant, [\xc9\xb9]. In extremely traditional and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative (linguistics)">conservative</a> accents (exemplified, for instance, in the speech of older speakers throughout the country, even in South-West Ireland, such as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%ADche%C3%A1l_%C3%93_Muircheartaigh" title="M\xc3\xadche\xc3\xa1l \xc3\x93 Muircheartaigh">M\xc3\xadche\xc3\xa1l \xc3\x93 Muircheartaigh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Healy-Rae" title="Jackie Healy-Rae">Jackie Healy-Rae</a>), the rhotic consonant, before a vowel sound, can also be an <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_tap" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar tap">alveolar tap</a>, [\xc9\xbe]. The rhotic consonant for the northern Ireland and new Dublin accents is a <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_approximant" title="Retroflex approximant">retroflex approximant</a>, [\xc9\xbb]. Dublin\'s retroflex approximant has no precedent outside of northern Ireland and is a genuine innovation of the past two decades. A <a href="/wiki/Guttural_R" title="Guttural R">guttural</a>/<a href="/wiki/Uvular" class="mw-redirect" title="Uvular">uvular</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xca\x81]</span> is found in north-east <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, the rhotic consonant of virtually all other Irish accents is the <a href="/wiki/Postalveolar_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Postalveolar approximant">postalveolar approximant</a>, [\xc9\xb9].\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_th"><b><a href="#ref_th">^4</a></b></span> The symbol [\xce\xb8\xcc\xa0] is used here to represent the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_non-sibilant_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless alveolar non-sibilant fricative">voiceless alveolar non-sibilant fricative</a>, sometimes known as a "slit fricative",<sup id="cite_ref-Harvcoltxt&#124;Hickey&#124;1984&#124;p=234_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvcoltxt|Hickey|1984|p=234-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> whose articulation is described as being <a href="/wiki/Apical_consonant" title="Apical consonant">apico</a>-<a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">alveolar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-de_Gruyter_2004_93_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-de_Gruyter_2004_93-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_hw"><b><a href="#ref_hw">^5</a></b></span> Overall, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hw/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/w/</span> are being increasingly <a href="/wiki/Wine%E2%80%93whine_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Wine\xe2\x80\x93whine merger">merged</a> in supraregional Irish English, for example, making <i>wine</i> and <i>whine</i> <a href="/wiki/Homophones" class="mw-redirect" title="Homophones">homophones</a>, as in most varieties of English around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-de_Gruyter_2004_93_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-de_Gruyter_2004_93-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other phonological characteristics of Irish English include that <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> ending in <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j/</span> before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span> are distinctive:<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j/</span> is dropped after <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Fricative">fricatives</a>, e.g. <i>new</i> sounds like <i>noo</i>, and <i>sue</i> like <i>soo</i>.</li>\n<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/dj/</span> becomes <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d\xca\x92/</span>, e.g. <i>dew/due, duke</i> and <i>duty</i> sound like "jew", "jook" and "jooty".</li>\n<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/tj/</span> becomes <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t\xca\x83/</span>, e.g. <i>tube</i> is "choob", <i>tune</i> is "choon"</li>\n<li>The following show neither dropping nor coalescence: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/kj/</span> (as in <i>cute</i>), <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/mj/</span> (as in <i>mute</i>), and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hj/</span> (as in <i>huge</i>; though the /h/ can be dropped in the South-West of Ireland).</li></ul>\n<p>The naming of the letter <i>H</i> as "haitch" is standard.\n</p><p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Gaelic</a> influence, an epenthetic <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a> is sometimes inserted, perhaps as a feature of older and less careful speakers, e.g. <i>film</i> [\xcb\x88f\xc9\xaal\xc9\x99m] and <i>form</i> [\xcb\x88f\xc9\x92\xcb\x90\xc9\xb9\xc9\x99m].\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loan_words_from_Irish">Loan words from Irish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Loan words from Irish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Irish_words_used_in_the_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish words used in the English language">number of Irish-language loan words</a> are used in Hiberno-English, particularly in an official state capacity.  For example, the head of government is the <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a>, the deputy head is the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A1naiste" title="T\xc3\xa1naiste">T\xc3\xa1naiste</a>, the parliament is the <a href="/wiki/Oireachtas" title="Oireachtas">Oireachtas</a> and its lower house is <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il_%C3%89ireann" title="D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann">D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann</a>. Less formally, people also use loan words in day-to-day speech, although this has been on the wane in recent decades and among the young.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable mw-collapsible mw-collapsed">\n<caption class="nowrap">Example loan words from Irish\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Word</th>\n<th>Part of speech</th>\n<th>Meaning\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Ab\xc3\xba</b></td>\n<td>Interjection</td>\n<td>Hooray! Used in sporting occasions, espec. for Gaelic games \xe2\x80\x93 \xc3\x81th Cliath ab\xc3\xba! \xe2\x80\x93 \'hooray for Dublin!\'\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Amad\xc3\xa1n</b><sup id="cite_ref-48" href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Interjection</td>\n<td>[To your] health!/Cheers!\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_words_from_Irish">Derived words from Irish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Derived words from Irish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Another group of Hiberno-English words are those <i>derived</i> from the Irish language. Some are <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Irish_origin" title="List of English words of Irish origin">words in English that have entered into general use</a>, while others are unique to Ireland. These words and phrases are often Anglicised versions of words in Irish or direct translations into English. In the latter case, they often give a meaning to a word or phrase that is generally not found in wider English use.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable mw-collapsible mw-collapsed">\n<caption Also a self-assertive worthless fellow".<sup id="cite_ref-oed_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Derived from a person who followed the Union Jack during British rule after 1801, a Dublin man who supported the crown. See <b>Shoneen</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Shoneen</b><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Seoin\xc3\xadn (diminutive of Sean \xe2\x80\x93 \'John\')</td>\n<td>An Irishman who imitates English ways \xe2\x80\x93 see <b>Jackeen</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Sleeveen</b><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Sl\xc3\xadbh\xc3\xadn</td>\n<td>An untrustworthy, cunning person\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Soft day</b><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Phrase</td>\n<td>L\xc3\xa1 bog <i>(lit.)</i></td>\n<td>Overcast day (light drizzle/mist)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_words_from_Old_and_Middle_English">Derived words from Old and Middle English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Derived words from Old and Middle English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Another class of vocabulary found in Hiberno-English are words and phrases common in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>, but which have since become obscure or obsolete in the modern English language generally. Hiberno-English has also developed particular meanings for words that are still in common use in English generally.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable mw-collapsible mw-collapsed">\n<caption class="nowrap">Example Hiberno-English words derived from Old and Middle English\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Word</th>\n<th>Part of speech</th>\n<th>Meaning</th>\n<th>Origin/notes\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Amn%27t" class="mw-redirect" title="Amn&#39;t">Amn\'t</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Verb</td>\n<td>Am not</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Childer</b><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Child</td>\n<td>Survives from Old-English, genitive plural of \'child\'<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Cop-on</b><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>shrewdness, intelligence, being \'street-wise\'<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Middle English from French <i>cap</i> \'arrest\'\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Craic</b> / <b>Crack</b><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Fun, entertainment. (e.g., "Devil take him")<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Negation (e.g., for none, "Devil a bit")<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a Northern England and Scotland)</td>\n<td>Middle English\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_words">Other words</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other words">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In addition to the three groups above, there are also additional words and phrases whose origin is disputed or unknown. While this group may not be unique to Ireland, their usage is not widespread, and could be seen as characteristic of the language in Ireland.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable mw-collapsible mw-collapsed">\n<caption class="nowrap">Example Hiberno-English words of disputed or unknown origin\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Word</th>\n<th>Part of speech</th>\n<th>Meaning</th>\n<th>Notes\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Acting the maggot</b><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Phrase</td>\n<td>Acting the fool, joking.</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Banjaxed</b><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Verb</td>\n<td>Broken, ruined, or rendered incapable of use. Still in everyday use, particularly in Dublin.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Messages</b></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Groceries" class="mw-redirect" title="Groceries">Groceries</a></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Minerals</b><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soft_drinks" class="mw-redirect" title="Soft drinks">Soft drinks</a></td>\n<td>From <a href="/wiki/Mineral_Waters" class="mw-redirect" title="Mineral Waters">mineral Waters</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Mot</b></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Girl or young woman, girlfriend</td>\n<td>From the Irish word \'maith\' meaning good, i.e. good-looking.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Press</b><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Cupboard</td>\n<td>Similarly, <i>hotpress</i> in Ireland means <i>airing-cupboard</i> Press is an old word for cupboard in Scotland and northern England.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Rake</b></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>many or a lot. Often in the phrase \'a rake of pints\'. Cf. Scots rake<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Runners</b><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Athletic_shoe" class="mw-redirect" title="Athletic shoe">Trainers/sneakers</a></td>\n<td>Also \'teckies\' or \'tackies\', especially in and around <a href="/wiki/Limerick" title="Limerick">Limerick</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Shore</b><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Stormdrain or Gutter. Cf. Scots shore<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Wet the tea</b><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>/<b>The tea is wet</b><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>Phrase</td>\n<td>Make the tea/the tea is made</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar_and_syntax">Grammar and syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Grammar and syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_syntax" title="Irish syntax">syntax of the Irish language</a> is quite different from that of English. Various aspects of Irish syntax have influenced Hiberno-English, though many of these idiosyncrasies are disappearing in suburban areas and among the younger population.\n</p><p>The other major influence on Hiberno-English that sets it apart from modern English in general is the retention of words and phrases from Old- and Middle-English.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Irish">From Irish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: From Irish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reduplication">Reduplication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Reduplication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">Reduplication</a> is an alleged trait of Hiberno-English strongly associated with <a href="/wiki/Stage_Irish" title="Stage Irish">Stage Irish</a> and Hollywood films.\n</p>\n<ul><li>the Irish <i>ar bith</i> corresponds to English "at all", so the stronger <i>ar chor ar bith</i> gives rise to the form "at all at all".\n<ul><li>"I\'ve no time at all at all."</li></ul></li>\n<li><i>ar eagla go \xe2\x80\xa6</i> (lit. "on fear that \xe2\x80\xa6") means "in case \xe2\x80\xa6". The variant <i>ar eagla na heagla</i>, (lit. "on fear of fear") implies the circumstances are more unlikely. The corresponding Hiberno-English phrases are "to be sure" and "to be sure to be sure". In this context, these are not, as might be thought, disjuncts meaning "certainly"; they could better be translated "in case" and "just in case". Nowadays normally spoken with conscious levity.\n<ul><li>"I brought some cash in case I saw a bargain, and my credit card to be sure to be sure."</li></ul></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yes_and_no">Yes and no</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Yes and no">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Irish lacks words that directly translate as <a href="/wiki/Yes_and_no" title="Yes and no">"yes" or "no"</a>, and instead <a href="/wiki/Echo_response" class="mw-redirect" title="Echo response">repeats the verb used in the question</a>, negated if necessary, to answer. Hiberno-English uses "yes" and "no" less frequently than other English dialects as speakers can repeat the verb, positively or negatively, instead of (or in redundant addition to) using "yes" or "no".\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Are you coming home soon?" \xe2\x80\x93 "I am."</li>\n<li>"Is your mobile charged?" \xe2\x80\x93 "It isn\'t."</li></ul>\n<p>This is not limited only to the verb <i>to be</i>: it is also used with <i>to have</i> when used as an auxiliary; and, with other verbs, the verb <i>to do</i> is used. This is most commonly used for intensification, especially in Ulster English.\n</p>\n<ul><li>"This is strong stuff, so it is."</li>\n<li>"We won the game, so we did."</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_past_construction">Recent past construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Recent past construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Irish indicates recency of an action by adding "after" to the present continuous (a verb ending in "-ing"), a construction known as the "hot news perfect" or "after perfect".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The idiom for "I had done X when I did Y" is "I was after doing X when I did Y", modelled on the Irish usage of the compound <a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">prepositions</a> <i>i ndiaidh</i>, <i>tar \xc3\xa9is</i>, and <i>in \xc3\xa9is</i>: <i>bh\xc3\xad m\xc3\xa9 tar \xc3\xa9is/i ndiaidh/in \xc3\xa9is X a dh\xc3\xa9anamh, nuair a rinne m\xc3\xa9 Y</i>.\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Why did you hit him?" \xe2\x80\x93 "He was after giving me cheek." (he had been cheeky to me).</li></ul>\n<p>A similar construction is seen where exclamation is used in describing a recent event:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"I\'m after hitting him with the car!" <i>T\xc3\xa1im tar \xc3\xa9is \xc3\xa9 a bhualadh leis an gcarr!</i></li>\n<li>"She\'s after losing five stone in five weeks!"</li></ul>\n<p>When describing less astonishing or significant events, a structure resembling the German perfect can be seen:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"I have the car fixed." <i>T\xc3\xa1 an carr deisithe agam.</i></li>\n<li>"I have my breakfast eaten." <i>T\xc3\xa1 mo bhricfeasta ithe agam.</i></li></ul>\n<p>This correlates with an analysis of "H1 Irish" proposed by Adger &amp; Mitrovic,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> in a deliberate parallel to the status of German as a <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">V2 language</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reflection_for_emphasis">Reflection for emphasis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Reflection for emphasis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The reflexive version of pronouns is often used for emphasis or to refer indirectly to a particular person, etc., according to context.  <i>Herself</i>, for example, might refer to the speaker\'s boss or to the woman of the house. Use of <i>herself</i> or <i>himself</i> in this way often indicates that the speaker attributes some degree of arrogance or selfishness to the person in question. Note also the indirectness of this construction relative to, for example, <i>She\'s coming now</i>\n</p>\n<ul><li>"\'Tis herself that\'s coming now." <i>Is \xc3\xad f\xc3\xa9in at\xc3\xa1 ag teacht anois.</i></li>\n<li>"Was it all of ye or just yourself?" <i>An sibhse ar fad n\xc3\xb3 tusa f\xc3\xa9in a bh\xc3\xad i gceist?</i></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prepositional_pronouns">Prepositional pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Prepositional pronouns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There are some language forms that stem from the fact that there is no verb <i>to have</i> in Irish. Instead, possession is indicated in Irish by using the preposition <i>at</i>, (in Irish, <i>ag.</i>). To be more precise, Irish uses a prepositional pronoun that combines <i>ag</i> "at" and <i>m\xc3\xa9</i> "me" to create <i>agam</i>.\nIn English, the verb "to have" is used, along with a "with me" or "on me" that derives from <i>T\xc3\xa1 \xe2\x80\xa6 agam.</i>  This gives rise to the frequent\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Do you have the book?" \xe2\x80\x93 "I have it with me."</li>\n<li>"Have you change for the bus on you?"</li>\n<li>"He will not shut up if he has drink taken."</li></ul>\n<p>Somebody who can speak a language "has" a language, in which Hiberno-English has borrowed the grammatical form used in Irish.\n</p>\n<ul><li>"She does not have Irish." <i>N\xc3\xadl Gaeilge aici.</i> literally "There is no Irish at her".</li></ul>\n<p>When describing something, many Hiberno-English speakers use the term "in it" where "there" would usually be used. This is due to the Irish word <i>ann</i> (pronounced "oun" or "on") fulfilling both meanings.\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Is it yourself that is in it?" <i>An t\xc3\xba f\xc3\xa9in at\xc3\xa1 ann?</i></li>\n<li>"Is there any milk in it?" <i>An bhfuil bainne ann?</i></li></ul>\n<p>Another idiom is this thing or that thing described as "this man here" or "that man there", which also features in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_English" title="Newfoundland English">Newfoundland English</a> in Canada.\n</p>\n<ul><li>"This man here." <i>An fear seo.</i> (cf. the related <i>anseo</i> = here)</li>\n<li>"That man there." <i>An fear sin.</i> (cf. the related <i>ansin</i> = there)</li></ul>\n<p>Conditionals have a greater presence in Hiberno-English due to the tendency to replace the simple present tense with the conditional (would) and the simple past tense with the conditional perfect (would have).\n</p>\n<ul><li>"John asked me would I buy a loaf of bread." (John asked me to buy a loaf of bread.)</li>\n<li>"How do you know him? We would have been in school together." (We went to school together.)</li></ul>\n<p><b>Bring</b> and <b>take</b>: Irish use of these words differs from that of British English because it follows the Irish grammar for <i>beir</i> and <i>t\xc3\xb3g</i>. English usage is determined by direction; person determines Irish usage. So, in English, one <b>takes</b> "<i>from</i> here <i>to</i> there", and <b>brings</b> it "<i>to</i> here <i>from</i> there". In Irish, a person <b>takes</b> only when accepting a transfer of possession of the object from someone else&#160;&#8211;&#32;and a person <b>brings</b> at all other times, irrespective of direction (to or from).\n</p>\n<ul><li>Don\'t forget to bring your umbrella with you when you leave.</li>\n<li>(To a child) Hold my hand: I don\'t want someone to take you.</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="To_be">To be</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: To be">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Irish equivalent of the verb "to be" has two present tenses, one (the present tense proper or "aimsir l\xc3\xa1ithreach") for cases which are generally true or are true at the time of speaking and the other (the habitual present or "aimsir ghn\xc3\xa1thl\xc3\xa1ithreach") for repeated actions. Thus, "you are [now, or generally]" is <i>t\xc3\xa1 t\xc3\xba</i>, but "you are [repeatedly]" is <i>b\xc3\xadonn t\xc3\xba</i>. Both forms are used with the verbal noun (equivalent to the English <a href="/wiki/Present_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Present participle">present participle</a>) to create compound tenses. This is similar to the distinction between <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ser" class="extiw" title="wikt:ser">ser</a></i> and <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/estar" class="extiw" title="wikt:estar">estar</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.\n</p><p>The corresponding usage in English is frequently found in rural areas, especially <a href="/wiki/County_Mayo" title="County Mayo">Mayo</a>/<a href="/wiki/County_Sligo" title="County Sligo">Sligo</a> in the west of Ireland and <a href="/wiki/County_Wexford" title="County Wexford">Wexford</a> in the south-east, Inner-City Dublin along with border areas of the North and Republic. In this form, the verb "to be" in English is similar to its use in Irish, with a "does be/do be" (or "bees", although less frequently) construction to indicate the continuous, or habitual, present:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"He does be working every day." <i>B\xc3\xadonn s\xc3\xa9 ag obair gach l\xc3\xa1.</i></li>\n<li>"They do be talking on their mobiles a lot." <i>B\xc3\xadonn siad ag caint go minic ar a bhf\xc3\xb3in p\xc3\xb3ca.</i></li>\n<li>"He does be doing a lot of work at school." <i>B\xc3\xadonn s\xc3\xa9 ag d\xc3\xa9anamh go leor oibre ar scoil.</i></li>\n<li>"It\'s him I do be thinking of." <i>Is air a bh\xc3\xadonn m\xc3\xa9 ag smaoineamh.</i></li></ul>\n<p>This construction also surfaces in <a href="/wiki/African_American_Vernacular_English" class="mw-redirect" title="African American Vernacular English">African American Vernacular English</a>, as the famous <a href="/wiki/Habitual_be" title="Habitual be">habitual be</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Old_and_Middle_English">From Old and Middle English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: From Old and Middle English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In old-fashioned usage, "it is" can be freely abbreviated <i>\xe2\x80\x99tis</i>, even as a standalone sentence. This also allows the double contraction <i>\xe2\x80\x99tisn\xe2\x80\x99t</i>, for "it is not".\n</p><p>Irish has separate forms for the second person singular (<i>t\xc3\xba</i>) and the second person plural (<i>sibh</i>).\nMirroring Irish, and almost every other <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_Languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European Languages">Indo European language</a>, the plural <i>you</i> is also distinguished from the singular in Hiberno-English, normally by use of the otherwise archaic English word <i>ye</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ji\xcb\x90]</span>; the word <i>yous</i> (sometimes written as <i>youse</i>) also occurs, but primarily only in Dublin and across <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>. In addition, in some areas in <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a>, north <a href="/wiki/Connacht" title="Connacht">Connacht</a> and parts of Ulster, the hybrid word <i>ye-s</i>, pronounced "yiz", may be used. The pronunciation differs with that of the northwestern being <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ji\xcb\x90z]</span> and the Leinster pronunciation being <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[j\xc9\xaaz]</span>.\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Did ye all go to see it?" <i>Ar imigh sibh go l\xc3\xa9ir chun \xc3\xa9 a fheicint?</i></li>\n<li>"None of youse have a clue!" <i>N\xc3\xadl ciall/leid ar bith agaibh!</i></li>\n<li>"Are ye not finished yet?" <i>Nach bhfuil sibh cr\xc3\xadochnaithe f\xc3\xb3s?</i></li>\n<li>"Yis are after destroying it!" <i>T\xc3\xa1 sibh tar \xc3\xa9is \xc3\xa9 a scriosadh!</i></li></ul>\n<p>The word <i>ye</i>, <i>yis</i> or <i>yous</i>, otherwise archaic, is still used in place of "you" for the second-person plural. <i>Ye\'r</i>, <i>Yisser</i> or <i>Yousser</i> are the possessive forms, e.g. "Where are yous going?"\n</p><p>The verb <i>mitch</i> is very common in Ireland, indicating being truant from school. This word appears in <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> (though he wrote in <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>), but is seldom heard these days in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>, although pockets of usage persist in some areas (notably <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a>, Devon, and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>). In parts of Connacht and Ulster the <i>mitch</i> is often replaced by the verb <i>scheme</i>, while in Dublin it is often replaced by "on the hop/bounce".\n</p><p>Another usage familiar from Shakespeare is the inclusion of the second person pronoun after the imperative form of a verb, as in "Wife, go you to her ere you go to bed" (<a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a>, Act III, Scene IV). This is still common in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>: "Get youse your homework done or you\'re no goin\' out!" In <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a>, you will still hear children being told, "Up to bed, let ye" <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l\xc9\x9b\xcb\x88t\xca\x83i]</span>.\n</p><p>For influence from <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Scots language">Ulster Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_English" title="Ulster English">Ulster English</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_grammatical_influences">Other grammatical influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other grammatical influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>Now</b> is often used at the end of sentences or phrases as a semantically empty word, completing an utterance without contributing any apparent meaning. Examples include "Bye now" (= "Goodbye"), "There you go now" (when giving someone something), "Ah now!" (expressing dismay), "Hold on now" (= "wait a minute"), "Now then" as a mild attention-getter, etc. This usage is universal among English dialects, but occurs more frequently in Hiberno-English.  It is also used in the manner of the Italian \'prego\' or German \'bitte\', for example a barman might say "Now, Sir." when delivering drinks.\n</p><p><b>So</b> is often used for emphasis ("I can speak Irish, so I can"), or it may be tacked onto the end of a sentence to indicate agreement, where "then" would often be used in Standard English ("Bye so", "Let\'s go so", "That\'s fine so", "We\'ll do that so"). The word is also used to contradict a negative statement ("You\'re not pushing hard enough" \xe2\x80\x93 "I am so!"). (This contradiction of a negative is also seen in American English, though not as often as "I am too", or "Yes, I am".) The practice of indicating emphasis with <i>so</i> and including reduplicating the sentence\'s subject pronoun and auxiliary verb (is, are, have, has, can, etc.) such as in the initial example, is particularly prevalent in more northern dialects such as those of Sligo, Mayo and the counties of Ulster.\n</p><p><b>Sure</b> is often used as a tag word, emphasising the obviousness of the statement, roughly translating as but/and/well. Can be used as "to be sure", the famous Irish stereotype phrase. (But note that the other stereotype of "Sure and \xe2\x80\xa6" is not actually used in Ireland.) Or "Sure, I can just go on Wednesday", "I will not, to be sure." The word is also used at the end of sentences (primarily in <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a>), for instance "I was only here five minutes ago, sure!" and can express emphasis or indignation.\n</p><p><b>To</b> is often omitted from sentences where it would exist in British English. For example, "I\'m not let go out tonight", instead of "I\'m not allowed <i>to</i> go out tonight".\n</p><p><b>Will</b> is often used where British English would use "shall" or American English "should" (as in "Will I make us a cup of tea?"). The distinction between "shall" (for first-person simple future, and second- and third-person emphatic future) and "will" (second- and third-person simple future, first-person emphatic future), maintained by many in England, does not exist in Hiberno-English, with "will" generally used in all cases.\n</p><p><b>Once</b> is sometimes used in a different way from how it is used in other dialects; in this usage, it indicates a combination of logical and causal conditionality: "I have no problem laughing at myself once the joke is funny."  Other dialects of English would probably use "if" in this situation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hiberno-English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul English</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> According to the 1841 census, Ireland had 8,175,124 inhabitants, of whom four million spoke <a href="/wiki/Irish_Gaelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Gaelic">Gaelic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline"
